Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
iii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
To supplement a growing need for proven knowledge regarding Neuroevolution Algo-
rithms, a suite of algorithms and problems were tested.
The algorithms implemented covered a range of approaches and featured different design
goals in the attempt of overcoming common diﬀiculties like fitness plateau traversal, local
optima, and overall providing good solutions as fast as possible. This included the (1+1)
Neuroevolution Algorithm (a neuroevolution parallel to (1+1)EA), a Local search based
algorithm, Self-Adaptive methodologies and others.
The set of problems tested were chosen with a similar goal of presenting a variety of chal-
lenges to the algorithms. The novel Hyper-Sphere Classification problems, were selected
due to their heavy theoretical foundation and static environment, while Cartpole, a staple
Reinforcement Learning problem, has a dynamic, temporal aspect grounded in physics.
The experiments were successful at highlighting different strengths and shortcomings,
and after statistical significance testing conclusions were taken.
The random distribution based approach taken by (1+1)NA, utilizing an Harmonic Dis-
tribution, brought good results across all tests. In tests with many parameters, a higher
probability of mutation proved to be a promising tool, especially in contexts where large
single-generation changes are valuable (e.g. Cartpole). Self-Adaptation as a concept
showed good results, but the specific design used (adaptive mutation strength for indi-
vidual parameters) was shown to be less effective in complex networks with a growing
number of parameters.

==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction
Neuroevolution, like many areas of machine learning, is growing quickly and spreading
throughout more and more industries. As these techniques become more common in
software development, a deep understanding of what makes a Neuroevolution system
right for the task at hand becomes necessary. However, in the same way other fields
in Artificial Intelligence have experienced, a lack of empirical data prevents this needed
understanding.
Often times, developers utilizing Neuroevolution are forced to make decisions made on
familiarity alone, as the field simply does not have the same bank of benchmarks, infor-
mation and results as other aspects of software engineering.
Thus, the goal of this thesis is to test a number of common concepts in the field of Neu-
roevolutionary Algorithms, with the intent of providing a better understanding on how they
perform in certain scenarios. A collection of algorithms and problems has been imple-
mented, representing many staple and state of the art concepts, as well as a variety of
contexts. This way, future design and application of Neuroevolution based solutions can
follow more rigorous hypothesis and assumptions.
This report will first explain some necessary background concepts in chapter 2. In chap-
ter 3, the solutions implemented are displayed in detail. The results of the experiments
executed using the algorithms and problems shown will be presented and analyzed in
chapter 4. Lastly, conclusions and insights are summarized in chapter 5, as well as pos-
sible vectors of further study.
As the core of this project, all algorithms, problems, and features required by these were
implemented using the Rust programming by the author. Additionally, the underlying neu-
ral network architecture was also designed and implemented from scratch, utilizing only
ndarray for matrix utilities and linear algebra tools, and rand & rand_distr for random value
generation using previously defined distributions (e.g. Uniform, Bernoulli, Standard Gaus-
sian) and the Rust standard libraries. The data processing and analysis shown were made
utilizing Microsoft Excel and Python, with the Scipy, Pandas and Numpy libraries.
A graphical user interface was built using Druid, to facilitate demonstration. A User Man-
ual of this application is found in appendix A.
All source code can be found at https://github.com/simoesd/implementation_na.

2
Background
For the full understanding of the matters shown in this report, some understanding of the
techniques used is required.
2.1
Feedforward Neural Networks
Feedforward neural networks are an essential building block in the field of machine learn-
ing. They allow for complex analysis of large amount of data, and are commonly used for
classification problems, where the network is tasked with correctly labeling a data set[1].
In machine learning, a neuron acts as the smallest information processing unit, and by
connecting multiple of these together, the network is able to interpret intricate informa-
tion. In regards to feedforward networks, neurons are organized into layers, and only
connections between adjacent layers are permitted. Other neural network architectures,
like Recurrent Neural Networks or Convolution Neural Networks, may organize neurons
and connections differently, but these techniques were not used in this paper.
Feedforward network include three types of layers:
1. The Input Layer defines the shape of the input the network accepts. Usually, each
input neuron corresponds to a feature in the provided data set. Only one of these
layers may exist in a feedforward neural network;
2. Hidden Layers are responsible for the majority of the work performed by the net-
work. Commonly, each neuron in an hidden layer has an activation function, which
processes the neuron’s output before it’s transferred to the next layer. Multiple hid-
den layers can exist, and are often necessary when working with certain problems.
3. The Output Layer defines the shape of the desired output. Neurons in the output
layer may also be equipped with an activation function (e.g. sigmoid for binary
classification problems). Only one output layer should be present.
The type of feedforward networks used throughout this paper are called Dense Neural
Networks, meaning every neuron in each layer is connected to all neurons in the sur-
rounding layers.
The flow of data in a neural network is referred to as a Forward Pass, and it starts at
the Input Layer, through all Hidden Layers, and finally through the Output Layer. During
the forward pass, information is processed using a series of matrix multiplications. Every
connection in a feedforward network has a weight. The value transferred through this
connection will be multiplied by the weight of the connection.
After each individual input of an hidden neuron is multiplied by its respective weight, all the
inputs are summed together. Additionally, each neuron may have a bias, to be summed
as well. In other words, a neuron with j connections will have j weights w1, w2, ..., wj and
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
3
==PAGE:11==
one bias b. With inputs x1, x2, ..., xj, the neurons output z is defined as:
z = x1w1 + x2w2 + ... + xjwj + b
(2.1)
If the neuron has an activation function, the calculated output z is passed through the
function, and the result is then sent to the next layer.

outputs
Activation functions are a powerful tool in machine learning, introducing non-linearity to
the system. They enable the network to model more complex patterns in the data. Some
examples include the Logistics function σ(x) (a special case of the Sigmoid function),
which transforms values in R into values in [−1, 1], or the Rectified Linear Unit (ReLu),
which returns the output value if it’s strictly positive, and 0 otherwise.
σ(x) =
1
1 + e−x
relu(x) = x + |x|
2
In this project’s implementation, neural networks were represented by the connections
between each layer. As all neurons followed the same process, no individual distinction
between them was required. As such, a layer is implemented as a two-dimensional matrix
of the weights in this layer, where each value corresponds to the weight of a connection
between neurons, and a list of biases, one per neuron in the layer.
2.2
Reinforcement Learning
Reinforcement Learning is a branch of machine learning, focused on decision making. An
agent (usually a neural network) is tasked with generating the best possible sequence of
decisions (called a policy) in a proposed challenge (e.g. the sequence of steps to take
when solving a maze).[2] A sequence of decisions is graded according to a reward func-
tion, designed for the problem at hand. An agent may be provided with this reward function
or a more limited version (often called a value function) as a way to estimate the likelihood
4
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:12==
of success of each decision it can take.
While some agents may be given full awareness of the environment[3], it’s also common
to only provide them with a limited amount of look-ahead capabilities. The neural network
is then able to calculate the value of as many steps as possible given the information it
has access to, and will choose the one that leads to the highest cumulative reward.
In the maze example, this could be only providing information about whether each di-
rection directly leads to a path or wall (no look-ahead), providing information of a certain
radius around the current position (limited look-ahead) or providing the whole layout of
the maze (omniscience). An adequate reward function could be the shortest valid path
from the agents current position to the exit of the maze. A value function, could instead
be the Manhattan distance to the exit. This value function is less reliable than the reward
function as it doesn’t take it account walls, but may be preferable if the goal of the project
is identifying the path throughout a new, unknown maze.
While not directly studied in this paper, staple Reinforcement Learning problems were
presented to our neural networks. These can be found in section 3.2.
2.3
Evolutionary Algorithms
Evolutionary Algorithms (EA) are a subset of optimization algorithms that attempt to sim-
ulate evolution in nature. A population of candidate solution are maintained, and through
each generation these solutions are evaluated, selected, and mutated. The goal is that
after each generation, the overall evaluation of the candidate population improves, even-
tually leading to a satisfactory solution being found. Each candidate solution can be en-
coded to a genotype, akin to the genetic material of a living being, which allows complete
recreation of the solution[4]. The process employed by EA can be described as:
1. Initialization: In the first generation, an initial population of candidates must be cre-
ated. This may be done for example, through random generation, utilizing heuristics
etc.;
2. Evaluation: Each candidate solution is given a fitness score according to a function
designed for the problem at hand;
3. Selection: The best candidate solutions (determined by the fitness score) are cho-
sen to be maintained while the rest are discarded. These solutions will also be used
in the next step to derive the candidates that will replace the discarded solutions.
Enforcing that a portion of the population is always replaced ensures that the popu-
lation changes across generations, preventing stagnation;
4. Evolution: Utilizing the candidates selected in step 2, a number of new solutions will
be created. This may be done utilizing any number of approaches. Two example are
Crossover, where parts of the genotype of multiple parent solutions are mixed to-
gether, or Mutation, where individual sections of the parents genotype are changed.
Multiple evolution approaches can be combined, and the details of how these ap-
proaches operate (e.g. how genotypes are combined in Crossover or what/how
sections are changed in Mutation) are dependent on the algorithm in question;
This sequence of steps (excluding initialization) is repeated throughout many generations,
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
5
==PAGE:13==
progressively evaluating, selecting and evolving the candidate solutions, until a termina-
tion criteria is met. This criteria might be a maximum number of generations, a certain
stagnation threshold, or the desired fitness score being achieved.
The size of the population maintained, how many candidate solutions are generated and
replaced each generation, and how evolution is executed are different from algorithm to
algorithm. Additionally, indirect encoding may be used. Direct/Indirect encoding refers to
whether the genotype of a solution can directly recreate the solution, or if further inter-
pretation is needed. Indirect encoding is often used for compression purposes, allowing
each solution to be stored in less information, or search-space mapping, where the solu-
tion parameters have direct relations to concepts in the problem being studied.
2.3.1
Neuroevolution
Neuroevolution is a field in machine learning that utilizes Evolutionary Algorithm tech-
niques to train neural networks. Each candidate solution is represented by a network, and
evolution is focused on updating the networks with the goal of finding a better solution[5].
The process described above has a direct parallel in machine learning, where Evaluation
can be compared to the forward pass and subsequent loss calculation, and the Selection
and Evolution steps are instead performed through procedures such as Back Propagation
with Stochastic Gradient Descent[6]. In Neuroevolution however, the principles of Evalu-
ation through a fitness function, and Selection and Evolution through Mutation Algorithms
are preferred.
Neuroevolution Algorithms (NA) can utilize different approaches to achieve their solutions:
• Fixed vs Adaptive Network Topology: Many neuroevolutionary algorithms mu-
tate exclusively the parameters of the network, and operate under a fixed topol-
ogy, meaning the network’s neurons and connections do not change after initializa-
tion (often called conventional neuroevolution algorithms). Other algorithms (e.g.
NEAT[7]) will not only mutate the values of the parameters of the network, but may
add or remove neurons or connections between neurons;
• Population sizes: Different NA algorithms will make use of different population
sizes. This defines how many neural networks are maintained each generation;
• Evolution approach: Like in Evolutionary Algorithms, each NA may utilize different
techniques to generate new candidates. Crossover and Mutation are once again
commonly utilized;
• Probability of mutation: When creating new candidates, the probability of muta-
tion will determine the amount of differences between a new network and it’s parent.
This can be represented by the likelihood of mutating a parameter, how many can-
didates are replaced generation by generation, or how often should new neurons or
connections be added or removed;
• Mutation strength: Once a mutation happens, the mutation strength will determine
how large the differences are between the parent network and the newly created
candidate. This is most applicable in the case of parameter mutation, where a larger
mutation strength can for example determine what value is added to the parameter
in question;
6
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:14==
Direct and Indirect encoding is also a relevant concept in Neuroevolution. Direct encoding
would mean that the mutation algorithms used are directly changing the networks param-
eters or structure (called the phenotype). On the other hand, Indirect encoding will involve
a method of transforming the genotype into a phenotype, from which the network can be
generated. Mutations in indirectly encoded genotypes will not directly correspond to mu-
tations in the phenotype (e.g. they may be attenuated/aggravated, a single change in the
genotype may involve multiple changes in the phenotype).
2.4
Statistical analysis
To be able to confidently take conclusions from the experiments ran, statistical tests were
used on the results. Statistical testing is based on proving or contradicting a null hypoth-
esis, that states that differences between two sets of data are caused by chance, and no
causality can be stated.
The most commonly used methods of statistical testing are parametric tests. Parametric
tests are tests where the sample values to be tested can be assumed to follow a specified
distribution with specified parameters. For example, the Student’s t-test[8] defines a sta-
tistical test where both samples follow a generalized Standard Gaussian distribution, and
share similar means and variance. If the variance can not be guaranteed to be the same
in both samples, an unequal variances t-test[9] can be used.
As the no initial information about the distribution of the data we will be analyzing can be
stated, we will instead utilize the Mann-Whitney U-test[10].
2.4.1
Mann-Whitney U-test
The Mann-Whitney U-test allows up to do comparative ordinal analysis between two sam-
ples, which in our case will be the number of generations needed in the execution of
different neuroevolution algorithms. As the samples to be compared are completely inde-
pendent from each other and may not follow a specific distribution other common statistical
tests (like the above mentioned Student’s t-test or Welch’s t-test) are not adequate, so this
test was a natural choice.
Each test provides us with two values: the U statistic, representing the overall result, and
a p-value representing the likelihood of obtaining the tested results if the null-hypothesis
is true. In other words, the lower the p-value, the more confident we can be on the con-
clusions taken are correct, as the probability that the emerging pattern is only visible due
to chance is lower.
The U statistic represents the difference between values in both samples. When testing
two samples S1 and S2, the U statistic can have a minimum value of 0 and a maximum
value of |S1| ∗|S2|.

Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
7
==PAGE:15==
The method to calculate the U statistic, and why the test may also be called a rank-sum,
is done by:
1. Combining both samples into a single, sorted collection;
2. A rank is attributed to each value based on their position in this collection, with 1
being attributed to the lowest value, 2 to the second lowest etc. If identical values
are present, the same rank should be attributed to all of them. This rank is equal to
the median of the positions of these identical results;
3. The ranks of each sample are summed. The result of this sum is the U statistic of
the set.
As we will performing these tests on the number of generations for two algorithms, a lower
U statistic represents an overall better performance of the first algorithm as it indicates the
algorithm was generally able to terminate at an earlier generation.

3
Design and Implementation
3.1
Mutation Algorithms
As explained in section subsection 2.3.1, Neuroevolution attempts to simulate evolution
in nature. The core of this are the Mutation Algorithms used. The mutation algorithms
studied in this project are conventional neuroevolution algorithms, with a fixed network
topology in mind. Looking into conventional neuroevolution algorithms exclusively allows
us to make stronger conclusions, as most parameters in each algorithm have an equiv-
alent in all the others. Adaptive-topology algorithms are incredibly promising, but have a
much broader scope that would require a different, brand new set of experiments with little
parallel to the ones studied here.
The algorithms presented mostly differ in approach regarding the mutation strength (when
a parameter is changed, how large is the change expected to be), but other differences
exist.
3.1.1
Standard Gaussian Mutation Algorithm
The Standard Gaussian Mutation algorithm serves a role as a very simple algorithm, to
allow us to look at the different approaches other algorithms utilize.
After each generation, it will attempt to mutate, on average, a single parameter per layer
of the network. When a parameter is drawn for mutation, it will be changed by a value
X ∈R drawn according to a Standard Normal distribution with a mean parameter µ = 0
and standard deviation parameter σ = 1.
To maintain the simplicity of this approach, no further alterations to the drawn mutation
strength (e.g. discretization) are made. With the number of neurons for a layer i Ni, the
weight of a neuron j in layer i wij, the algorithm can be described as:

3.1.2
(1+1) Neuroevolution Algorithms
In [11], the (1+1) Neuroevolution Algorithm (refferred to as (1+1)NA) was proposed, with
a discretization of the search space in mind. This algorithm is a direct reference to the
classic Evolutionary Algorithm (1+1)EA, where every generation one solution is kept (the
first ”1” in the name), and one new one is created (the second ”1”). If this new solution is
as good or better than the original, it’s kept for the next generation. Otherwise, the original
solution continues forward.
(1+1)NA is a direct application of this principle to the landscape of neuroevolution, main-
taining one neural network per generation, and mutating it once. The mutated network is
kept if its fitness score is as good or better than the original.
The originally proposed (1+1)NA algorithms map the networks parameters directly to the
search space of the problem, as the Algorithm was designed in parallel with the Hyper-
Sphere Classification problems presented in subsection 3.2.2. The goal of this project is
to evaluate the merit of the results for more widely applicable situations, so this mapping
was not utilized. While separating the algorithms implementation from the problem results
in some of the original goals being lost, we consider the value gained from testing the al-
gorithms and problems in a more diverse environment as most important in the spirit of
this project.
Weights and biases of the network are mutated by steps of length 1
r, with r being a resolu-
tion parameter. For example, a parameter described in the continuous domain [0, 1] would
instead be considered to live in the discrete domain {0, 1
r, 2
r, ...1}. Due to the decreased
granularity of the search space, a true global optimum might no longer be achievable due
to the , so a tolerance is allowed. For a maximization problem with a fitness domain in
[0, 1] (e.g. the fraction of inputs correctly classified), a solution s would be considered
optimal if f(s) ≥1 −1
r.

The proposed (1+1) NA algorithm attempts to mutate each parameter with an indepen-
dent probability of
1
2N , with N being the number of neurons in the network.
Two such algorithms were presented.
(1+1)NA with Local mutation
The (1+1)NA algorithm with local mutation (referred to as Local (1+1)NA) maintains the
fixed step size of 1
r, and further fixes the number of steps per mutation at 1.
A local search is a common approach in optimization problems, as a simple and generally
consistent solution to many computationally hard problems. Local searches are based
on defining neighbour solutions, or solutions with minimal changes from the current best
solution, and at each timestep the algorithm will test one of these neighbouring solutions.
Local searches often do not guarantee an optimal solution, and are in fact particularly
susceptible to stagnating at a local optimum, as in these cases no neighbouring solution
will be an improvement, and thus no change can be made.
In Local (1+1)NA, neighbouring solutions are defined parameter-wise. In other words,
neighbour networks may have any number of differing parameters, but the difference be-
tween a parameter in the two networks must not be larger than 1
r. In each timestep, each
parameter in the network will have an independent probability of
1
2N to be mutated. When
a parameter is to be mutated, a random variable σ ∈{−1, 1} is drawn uniformly, and σ
r is
added to the parameters value.

(1+1)NA with Harmonic distribution
The other variant of the (1+1)NA proposed is the (1+1)NA with Harmonic distribution
(henceforth referred to simply as (1+1)NA). In contrast to Local (1+1)NA, instead of mu-
tating a parameter by a fixed step of size 1

an Harmonic distribution and mutates the parameter by x
r .

The harmonic distribution is used to eschew certain issues found in other common algo-
rithms:
• Local Optima: the harmonic distribution is a heavy-tailed distribution, meaning
drawing high values isn’t common but still reasonably likely.
This alleviates the
possibility of stagnation at a local optimum by allowing the network to suffer large
changes in a given timestep.
• Large plateau traversal: Large plateaus often pose big issues for neuroevolution
algorithms with low mutation strengths. A plateau is defined as an section of neigh-
bour solutions with the exact same fitness score. Because of this, the network can
be mutated back to an already visited state, and consistent progression can not be
guaranteed. When this happens, the mutation algorithm’s behavior can be com-
pared to an n-dimensional random walk, where each neuron corresponds to a di-
mension. By allowing larger mutation strengths, plateaus can be searched much
quicker, reducing the possibility of stagnation
• Precise optima: More aggressive mutation strengths can facilitate the convergence
of a network in an optimum. However, they increase the tolerance necessary for a
solution to be considered optimal due to smaller granularity. By being prone to over-
shoot the true global optimum, these networks may be unable to approach optima
as closely as less aggressive algorithms. With the harmonic distribution, lower val-
ues are significantly more likely to be generated than higher ones, allowing us to
maintain a lower tolerance as (1+1)NA can consistently find global optimum once it
is in the vicinity
12
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:20==

3.1.3
Self Adaptive Mutation Strength
Self Adaptive neuroevolution algorithms refer to a family of algorithms that mutate both
the neural-network parameters and the meta-parameters of the algorithm (e.g. mutation
strength, probability of mutating a parameters). A common self adjusting neuroevolu-
tion algorithm proposed focuses on adapting the mutation strength, (i.e. by how much a
parameter is changed), based on the success or failure of previous mutation attempts[12].
As self adaptation has been applied to a variety of different algorithms and evolution strate-
gies, variations of both the algorithms explained in subsection 3.1.2 were implemented
utilizing an additional Self-Adaptive Mutation strength module. The approached taken in
our implementation creates a mutation strength value for each parameter (weights and
biases) in the neural network. Every mutation to be made to each parameter is instead
multiplied by the respective mutation strength before being added to the parameter. Af-
ter a mutation is finished, the mutation strengths of every parameter involved is updated
depending on the success of the mutation (increased on strict success, decreased other-
wise). The mutation strength of each parameter has a minimum of 1.
This self adaptation strategy has the following goals in mind:
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
13
==PAGE:21==
1. Fast Convergence: By causing larger mutations in the case of repeated success,
self-adaptive mutation strength excels at ”hill-climbing”, or the process of converging
on optima once in the vicinity. If a neighbouring solution has strictly better fitness,
future mutations with that parameter will be more aggressive. When our neural
network is in the correct path, and there is a sequence of neighbouring solutions
leading to an optimum, this sequence can quickly be traversed utilizing this higher
mutation strength value.
2. Precise Optima: After arriving at the close vicinity of an optimum, fewer mutations
will result in success. This leads to the relevant mutation strengths to be reduced, al-
lowing for fine-tuning of the result and avoiding repeated overshooting of the precise
optimum.
The self-adaptation module can be described as:
Notation
Meaning
xt
Neural network at time step t
f(x)
Fitness score of a network x
s
Mutation strength success multiplier
d
Mutation strength failure multiplier
Table 3.4: Notation used in algorithm 4
Algorithm 4 (1+1) Neuroevolution Algorithm with Harmonic Mutation
for each parameter pi in x do
mi ←1
end for
while the termination criteria is not met do
y ←xt
Mutate y according to the chosen mutation algorithm
Store what parameters were mutated
if f(y) > f(xt) then
for each mutated parameter pi do
pi ←max(1, pi ∗s)
end for
else
for each mutated parameter pi do
pi ←max(1, pi ∗d)
end for
end if
end while
Due to the nature of the Self-Adaptation approach used, some of the discretization details
were lost. While the original mutation value fit into the 1
r-discretized domain, the self-
adaptive strength multiplier can result in a non-compliant mutation value. For example,
using Local (1+1)NA, a parameter with a self-adaptive strength of 1.7 would be mutated
by 1.7
r .

3.2
Problems
To evaluate the algorithms, a few problems were chosen. Cartpole, a common bench-
mark used in reinforcement learning, and a set of Hyper-sphere classification problems,
proposed in [11]. While relatively simple, these problems can prove to be complicated
to solve, and were chosen for their differences. Cartpole has been a staple problem for
decades and provides a dynamic environment, grounded in physics, where the network
is tested multiple times in each generation. The Hyper-sphere problems however, repre-
sent a novel, static state, with more theoretical roots, more akin to the classic OneMax
optimization problem.
3.2.1
Cartpole
The Cartpole problem, formally described by Barto et al.[13] in 1983, is a commonly used
challenge for reinforcement learning systems as a diﬀicult control problem.
It consists of a two dimensional environment, where a pole is connected to a cart at its
base. The goal is to keep the pole balanced by moving the cart left or right. The network
is evaluated over multiple time steps, until a termination condition is fulfilled:
1. The angle between the pole and the vertical axis is greater than a set limit. If this
occurs, the pole is considered unbalanced and the problem finishes as a failure;
2. The distance between the center point of the problem and the cart is greater than a
set limit. If this occurs the problem finishes as a failure;
3. None of the failure conditions detailed above occurred over a certain number of time
steps. If this occurs, the problem finishes as a success.
The balance of the pole in the implemented version of the problem follows simple kine-
matics rules. The pole’s angle is changed at the end of a timestep by a calculated angular
velocity. This angular velocity is in turn updated by an angular acceleration affected by
gravity and by the horizontal movement of the cart. This represents both the challenge
to overcome, and the method for which the system can solve the problem. The cart is
considered fixed at the vertical axis, and thus no gravity is applied to it. Horizontally, it
moves based on forces applied by the system. Each timestep, an output is generated
by the neural network, henceforth called acceleration. The cart’s acceleration affects it’s
velocity, which represents the change in position at the end of the timestep. Cart acceler-
ation is not maintained between time steps, while the pole angle, angular velocity, angular
acceleration, cart position and cart velocity are kept.
This representation was chosen as it creates a truly persistent environment, where the
decisions taken in earlier time steps not only have an immediate effect, possibly tipping
the pole out of balance, but also affect the system in subsequent time steps through the
pole’s angular velocity, acceleration and the cart’s velocity. This avoids the possibility of
balancing the cart by arriving at a balanced point early on and not moving the cart any
further.
Our network will receive 5 inputs (the above mentioned persistent variables) and will gen-
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
15
==PAGE:23==
erate a single output, representing the force to be applied to the cart in the current timestep.
The system does not have any other insight into the rules governing the problem, like the
equations of motion used. The fitness score of a solution is represented by the number
of time steps before termination.
Two variants of this problem, both discrete, were tested:
1. N-Steps, where the output value of the network is rounded to an integer, corre-
sponding to the number of steps to take in each timestep. A negative number of
steps moves the cart to the left, and a positive number of steps moves it to the right.
In each time step, at least one step will be taken. The step size used was 10
r , with r
being a resolution parameter.
2. Discretized, where the output value of the network is rounded to the nearest multiple
of a set step size. The step size used was 0.6.
The step sizes picked needed to be large enough for the system to balance the pole and
small enough to allow fine control, but were otherwise chosen arbitrarily. The initial cart
position was in the range [−5, 5], randomly drawn from a uniform distribution, and the
initial angle of the pole was in the range [−0.01, 0.01], in radians, also uniformly drawn.
For each network, these random values were seeded, to allow for consistent evaluation
between generations.
3.2.2
Hyper-sphere Classification Problems
The hyper-sphere classification problems refer to a set of challenges were our network is
tasked with correctly classifying a section of an hyper-sphere. For each problem, an in-
terval of angles is considered positive, and the network needs to generate an hyper-plane
to separate the positive section from the rest. This generated hyper-plane is represented
by a vector, using a polar coordinate system. For n dimensions, the vector is represented
by n −1 angles and 1 bias. The angles are bounded in the domain [0, 2π] and the bias is
bounded in [−1, 1] utilizing an extended modulo function referred to as ring. Results will
”loop around” the bound, behaving the same as the standard modulo operator in Rust if
the value is positive and the bound is [0, n], but allowing for further defined behavior for
negative values and, if desired, a symmetric domain [−n, n]. For example with the value
-0.2 and the domain [0, 1], ring(−0.2, 0, 1) = 0.8.
ring(x, 0, M) =
{
x%M,
for x ≥0
M + (x%M),
for x < 0
(3.2)
ring(x, −M, M) =
{
(x + M)%2M −M
for x ≥0
(|x| + M) ∗sign(x)%(M ∗2.0) + M
for x < 0
(3.3)
Equations 3.2.2 and3.2.2 describe the ring function for traditional and symmetric limits,
respectively.
Multiple sections of the sphere may be positive for a specific problem. In these cases
for a solution to be optimal, positive points must be classified as positive by at least one
hyper-plane, while negative points must be classified as negative by every hyper-plane.
Fitness is evaluated based on how much of the hyper-sphere was considered correctly
classified.

11. 4-dimensional Hyper-sphere two quarters, opposite of each other. Positive angle
intervals {[0, π], [0, π], [0, π]} ∪{[π, 2π], [0, π], [0, π]}. Tested with 2 output vectors.
The positive intervals described above follow a simple structure. Each interval corre-
sponds to a pair of angles (minimum and maximum), and each positive section is rep-
resented as a set of intervals, one per angle required (n-dimensions require n−1 angles).
Then, in problems where multiple separate positive sections exist (e.g. 2D Sphere Local
Optima or 3D Sphere Two Slices), a union of all sections is made.
Figure 3.1: Visualization of the 2-Dimensional Hyper-Sphere problems. From left to right:
Quarter, Half, Two Quarters, and Local Optima
Continuous variant for two dimensions
A continuous implementation for two dimensions was created. Here, we can calculate the
intersection of our hyper-sphere (a circumference for 2 dimensions) and the half-space
above each generated hyper-plane (a line, in 2 dimensions). This intersection can be
represented as a pair of angles, corresponding to the arc between the two points where
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
17
==PAGE:25==
the line intersects the circumference. Using polar coordinates, the vector v = [θ, b] can be
transformed to an arc [m, M] as:
m = ring(θ −arccos(b), 0, 2π)
M = ring(θ + arccos(b), 0, 2π)
(3.4)
Figure 3.2: Representation of the vector to arc transformation
It’s important to note that if our network is classifying an arc that crosses the x-axis be-
tween the first and fourth quadrants, the output of the ring function will follow m < M. In
these cases, two arcs will be generated instead, [0, M] and [m, 2π], allowing us to keep
all angles in the [0, 2π] domain.
Finally, we can utilize the positive ranges provided by the problem, and the arc gener-
ated by the network, to calculate the length of the correctly classified circumference. This
length, in the domain [0, 2π], can be normalized to [0, 1], corresponding to the fraction
of correctly classified circumference. Assuming the given positive ranges as G, and the
ranges classified as positive by the network as N we can obtain the set of positively clas-
sified ranges C:
C = (N ∩G) ∪(N ∩G)
(3.5)
Considering C = [m1, M1] ∪[m2, M2]∪...∪[mj, Mj], the fitness score f can be calculated
as:
f =
j
∑
n=1
Mn −mn
2π
(3.6)
This approach does not correctly scale to two dimensions, as the hyper-space generated
by n hyper-plane in D > 2 can’t always be represented as a collection of pairs of angles.
Instead, the section classified by the hyper-plane corresponds to a hyper-spherical cap,
while the positive angle ranges can represent an hyper-spherical cap, hyper-spherical
orthant or other varieties of shapes. This behavior is shown for 3 Dimensions in 3.3.

Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:26==
Figure 3.3: Visualization of the spherical octant represented in 3-dimensions Sphere Cor-
ner on the left, and a spherical cap created by a plane on the right. While the shown cap
is the best possible solution, 1.0 fitness is not possible.
Discrete variant for higher dimensions
A discrete implementation for two and higher dimensions was created. Here, the fitness
of a network is calculated by classifying a number of randomly generated uniformly dis-
tributed points in the surface of our n-sphere. As experiments with dimensions above 3
were conducted equidistant point generation wasn’t possible and a high number of uni-
formly distributed points was preferred. It is assumed that for any x, a randomly selected
section corresponding to x% of the sphere’s surface will contain approximately x% of the
points.
The sampling of points was made using a generalized version of the Box-Muller trans-
form[14] proposed by Muller[15] and Marsaglia[16]. This is necessary as simply drawing
n−1 uniformly distributed angles in [0, 2π] results in a higher density of points at the poles
of an n-dimensional hyper-sphere.
Algorithm 5 Hyper-sphere Point Picking algorithm generating x points in n dimensions
Require: x and n are positive integers
N(0, 1) generates a random variable following a Standard Gaussian Distribution
i ←0
while i < x do
Sample n values (p1, ..., pn) ∼N(0, 1)
r ←
√∑n
j=1 p2
j
Return the point ( p1
r , ..., pn
r )
end while
We can then calculate whether a point is above or on the solution hyper-plane. Each
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
19
==PAGE:27==
network output corresponds to a vector, which can be transformed into an orthogonal
hyper-plane splitting our environment into two half-spaces. A point is considered above
the hyper-plane if it’s in the half-space the output vector points to. Utilizing Cartesian
coordinates, a point p = [x1, ..., xn], a normal unit vector v = [v1, ..., vn], and the bias of
the vector b, the point is above or on the line if:
v · p −v · (v ∗b) ≥0
(3.7)
In the first part of the equation, a dot product between the vector and the point is done. If
the result is equal to 0, the point is orthogonal to the vector, and if the value is greater than
0 it’s in the half-space the vector points towards. This initial part assumes the hyper-plane
is centered at the origin. As this may not always be the case, the bias is used to calculate
the center of the plane instead.
Once again, for a positive point (a point within the ranges provided by the problem) to be
correctly classified it should be above or on at least one of the output hyper-planes, while
a negative point must be below every output hyper-plane.
An optimal fitness of 1.0 is not possible due to the same reasons a discrete represen-
tation was needed, described at the end of subsubsection 3.2.2. If the positive ranges
do not describe an hyper-spherical cap, complete separation of the positive and negative
sections of the hyper-sphere can’t be generated with an hyper-plane. In these cases, the
achievable optimum will fall between completely covering the range, or being completely
covered by the range, i.e. circumscribed or inscribed in a 2-dimensional projection.

4
Experiments and results
An extensive suite of experiments was conducted, utilizing all the problems and muta-
tion algorithms presented in chapter 3. A standard significance level of 0.05 from Mann-
Whitney U Tests was used to establish confidence in the presented results. As the Mann-
Whitney U results focus on the number of generations before an execution is terminated,
a lower U Statistic indicates earlier successes from the first algorithm.
Different parameters were tested, including:

Resolution parameters above 240 were not tested for problems beyond the 2D continuous
Hyper-sphere. Since results above this value did not display significant differences and
high resolution parameters extended the execution time of the experiments significantly,
they were skipped for subsequent experiments to accommodate for the time frame allot-
ted project delivery.
For each problem, the number of inputs was changed to fit the problem inputs, and the
number of outputs changed to fit the shape and number of outputs desired. When testing
Cartpole problems, the network was provided with the pole angle, pole angular velocity,
pole angular acceleration, cart position and cart velocity as described in subsection 3.2.1.
In the hyper-sphere problems, the network was provided with the positive ranges, de-
scribed in subsection 3.2.2.
All networks utilized bias, and all weights and biases were initialized using a uniform ran-
dom distribution in the range [−0.1, 0.1]. These initial values were discretized by 1
r, as
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
21
==PAGE:29==
described in subsection 3.1.2. While activation functions are an important tool in machine
learning, introducing non-linearity, the problems utilized did not require a non-linear land-
scape. Thus they were not used, allowing us to more clearly see the impacts of the tested
variables. However, the ability to utilize activation functions was implemented and is a
clear next step in this line of study.
This project worked exclusively with Fixed Topology algorithms utilizing direct encoding.
All algorithms will utilize a population size of one kept candidate and one new candidate
per generation. A maximum generation of 100r log(r) was set, at which point the execu-
tion was terminated.
The fitness score at which an execution is considered successful can be different for each
problem. The implemented Cartpole problem considered 30 time steps per second in its
gravity constant, and success was determined after 900 time steps, or 30 seconds. As
such, the optimal fitness score in these problems is 900.
The hyper-sphere problems considered fitness scores in the domain of [0, 1]:
1. For 2 dimensional hyper-sphere problems, we are only bounded by the number of
outputs desired:
• For Sphere Quarter, Sphere Half, Sphere Two Quarters with 2 outputs, and
Sphere Local Optimum with 3 outputs the optimal fitness score is 1.0;
• For Sphere Two Quarters with 1 output, and Sphere Local Optima with 1 output
the optimal fitness score is 0.75. In this case, Sphere Local Optima has a
possible local optima of fitness 2
3
2. For 3 and 4 dimensional hyper-sphere problems, not all inputs have a solution with
a fitness of 1.0 due to the problems described at the end of subsubsection 3.2.2.
The following values were obtained through experimentation:
• For 3D Sphere Half the optimal fitness score is 1.0, as a spherical cap is formed
by the positive range;
• For 3D Sphere Corner the optimal fitness score was 0.98;
• For 3D Sphere Slice the optimal fitness score was 0.91;
• For 3D Sphere Two Slices the optimal fitness score was 0.96;
• For 4D Hyper-sphere Half, Quarter, and Two Quarters, the optimal fitness score
is 0.921.
4.1
Results and Analysis
In total, 36200 neural networks were trained across these experiments, so only some rel-
evant results are shown found in this section. Further data can be found at the end of the
report in appendix B.
4.1.1
2-Dimensional Continuous Hyper-sphere problems
As seen in Table 4.2, a low number of networks in the Sphere Quarter problems, which
included no local optimum, got stuck at a fitness score of 0.75. All of these cases oc-
curred with the Local (1+1)NA and Self-Adaptive Local(1+1)NA (henceforth referred to as
22
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:30==
SA Local). After further investigation, we found that this was caused by large plateaus,
where by classifying the whole domain as negative (with a bias very close to 1) a fairly
high fitness score could be achieved. When this bias is set, the only further improvement
is obtained by adjusting the angle of vector closer to the optimum until some sections of
the positive range are above the line and only then can the bias be reduced again. Due to
the nature of the problem, this can involve moving the angle through a significant portion
of the search space, creating a large plateau.
As discussed in subsection 3.1.2, some algorithms struggle with large plateau traversal.
This traversal can be compared with a random walk with a number of dimensions equal
to the number of parameters to mutate. This reinforces the goal of the chosen distribution
for the (1+1) Neuroevolution Algorithm. The heavy-tailed distribution chosen succeeded
in allowing for faster traversal of these large plateaus, facilitating convergence at an op-
timum. The Gaussian mutation algorithm, while not heavy-tailed, has the possibility of
drawing higher mutation strengths, which allowed it to avoid this issue. As an attempt to
avoid this issue, further tests for the sphere continuous problems were ran allowing the
Self Adaptive module to increase mutation strength if the mutated fitness score was equal
or greater than the previous one (as opposed to strictly greater). These algorithms are
referred to as SA Local Eq. and SA (1+1)NA Eq.. This approach was successful at solving
this issue.

The SA (1+1)NA and (to a greater extent) the SA (1+1)NA with equality adaptation occa-
sionally show issues with finding precise optima. Since (1+1)NA is capable of generating
high mutation strengths, the added multiplier provided by the SA module often resulted
in mutations too large to be successful, in particular when in the vicinity in the optimum.
This is most apparent in problems with multiple outputs (Local Optima and Two Quarters),
where these algorithms did not inherit the success of the baseline (1+1)NA. Instead, they
display a median final score much higher than other algorithms with similarly low success
rates, and although outside the allowed tolerance, incredibly close to the optimum.
This same issue was found in SA Local with equality self-adaptation (see Table 4.2), where
in Sphere Quarter a few networks were not able to reach a score within tolerance despite
getting good solutions. By allowing the algorithm to quickly traverse the plateaus de-
scribed above, it was susceptible to arriving at the vicinity of the optimum with very high
mutation strengths, and not being able to reduce them before the generation limit was
reached. Although this situation was rare in the experiments ran, problems with even
larger plateaus could aggravate this.

a local optimum were Local (1+1) and its self-adaptive variants. As described in 3.1.2,
local search algorithms are vulnerable to local optima due to their limited number of muta-
tions. Self-adaptation, while effective at converging at an optimum quickly, did not make
a significant difference afterwards. Since improvements are scarcer the closer to optimal
the solution is, mutation strengths are reduced quickly to the set minimum, and become
identical to the baseline Local (1+1)NA algorithm.

For problems with multiple outputs (see tables 4.3 and 4.7), which include more network
parameters that can be mutated, (1+1)NA was the only algorithm with acceptable results.
The percentage of optimal solutions found was the lowest in these problems for all al-
gorithms, but (1+1)NA showed much higher success rates, with approximately a 90%
success rate in Sphere Two Quarters (compared to under 20% for all other algorithms)
and 38% in Sphere Local Optima (compared to under 5% for others). Overall, the causes
for these results for the three families of algorithms were:
1. (1+1)NA and its Self-Adaptive variants consistently reached good fitness scores (all
have median scores above 0.95). However, both self-adaptive versions consistently
failed at arriving at a score within the allowed tolerance. This also happened to the
baseline (1+1)NA problem, but significantly less often.
2. The Gaussian algorithm also reached fairly high fitness scores (a median of above
0.90 in Local Opt, and 0.98 in Two Quarters). However, it’s mutation distribution is
less likely to generate high values than (1+1)NA, resulting in diﬀiculties arriving close
to the optimum. It’s higher probability of mutating a parameter may have helped at
obtaining these fitness scores.
3. Local (1+1)NA consistently found an optimal vector, but was not able to find a good
solution with the others. This can easily be attributed to its low mutation strength,
as traversing the domain takes longer the lower this value is. The results were the
same with SA Local and SA Local Eq..

When presented with more parameters, the Self-Adaptation approach used proved less

more successful mutations are necessary to increase this value. Paired with local optima
created after one optimal vector is found, as further moving that vector will often reduce

the fitness score, the self adaptive algorithm’s behavior is much closer to their baseline
version than in simpler problems.
4.1.2
2-Dimensional Discrete Hyper-sphere problems
To preface the following sections, it’s important to note that due to the possible variance
in random point distribution, certain results may be slightly outside the optimal solutions
established for the continuous problems. For example. in the 2 Dimensional Discrete Two
Quarters problem with a single output, fitness scores slightly above 0.75 are possible. If
points are not perfectly split between the positive and negative ranges, an optimum above
0.75 is present.

As expected, the results from the 2-dimensional discrete problems repeat a lot of the same
insights obtained from the Continuous results presented above. A few key differences are
however present:
1. Across the board, median generation results were lower;
2. Only one network got stuck in the Local Optima present in the Sphere Local Optima
with 1 output problem;
3. (1+1)NA recorded a significantly higher success rate in the Sphere Local Optima
with 3 outputs problems;
4. All algorithms had a similar success rate in the Sphere Two Quarters with 2 outputs
problem.
5. All algorithms had an abnormally low median generation in the Sphere Local Optima
with 1 output problem.
While the first three observations can be attributed to the discretized environment having
only 1000 points, facilitating overall traversal, the last two points are unusual.
After looking into the debug logs, an issue with the random generator seeding in these
two experiments was noticed. The experiments were ran in a multi-threaded environment,
and early on in its execution the thread responsible for these two tests ran into an issue
with the random number generator in charge of creating the points in the sphere, causing
it to present the same points for every subsequent network trained. While this wouldn’t
necessarily cause issues, the selected seed had an abnormal point distribution, skewing
the results significantly. At the time this was found, there wasn’t enough time to rerun the

All of these experiments include a higher number of mutable parameters, and a few in-
sights can be gathered:
1. The (1+1)NA and SA (1+1)NA were the most consistent algorithms at finding the
global optimum in this high-parameter environment;
2. The Local (1+1)NA and SA Local’s performance is significantly hindered by a rising
number of parameters, with the percentage of networks reaching a global optimum
going down the more parameters were in the network;
3. The Gaussian mutation algorithm much more successful in these cases than antici-
pated. The Sphere Continuous problem results indicated that the Gaussian mutation
algorithm would struggle with larger networks. However, these issues seem to be
mostly caused by the plateaus present in the continuous environment. As traversal
is simplified in these discrete problems, the algorithm’s higher expected number of
mutations shows a greater impact.
4. Even in poorly performing cases, the median final scores were generally high.
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
27
==PAGE:35==
These tests indicate that passive algorithms, like Local (1+1)NA, struggle with larger
search spaces and networks when compared to algorithms with more aggressive mutation
approaches. The Self-Adaptive Mutation Strength while still useful, seems less effective in
these circumstances. On the other hand, random distribution based mutation algorithms
performed well, and the expected number of mutations per generation presents itself as
a possible improvement vector for complicated environments.

Table 4.13: Significance test result for Cartpole N-Steps for the Gaussian and Local
(1+1)NA algorithms.
The persistence of each Cartpole execution introduces an interesting challenge for neu-
roevolution. Neuroevolution generally follows a greedy approach, where a new solution is
only accepted if it’s at least as good as the current one. In the Cartpole problem, changes
in early time steps can prove beneficial in long run by allowing a more balanced state
later in the execution. However, due to the aforementioned greedy approach taken by
neuroevolution, these change have to be done within a single generation, as any change
that causes a failure earlier than the current best is immediately discarded. It’s likely that
the higher probability for mutation in the Gaussian Algorithm contributed in this regard,
allowing the fast changes required for a persistent problem of this kind.
While (1+1)NA achieved slightly lower optimality rates and slower solutions than the Gaus-
sian mutation algorithm, Mann-Whitney U tests did not consistently show a significant dif-
ference between them. In fact, for the r = 120 experiments, an appropriate significant
threshold was only seen in the Discretized problem, while for r = 240, this threshold was
only achieved in N-Steps version. (1+1)NA with the Self-Adaptive module maintained a
lower optimality rate than the baseline variant, but bolstered earlier solutions than even
the Gaussian algorithm. Once again, the significance of these solution speed differences
was inconsistent.

==PAGE:38==
5
Conclusion
The goal of this project was to collect and analyze empirical data regarding Neuroevolution
Algorithms. With this purpose, a few algorithms were selected, and a suite of experiments
was designed. A significant amount of information was produced allowing for confident
statements regarding the implemented solutions.
The two different kinds of problems implement chose to highlight strengths and shortcom-
ings of proposed solutions.
While Local Search algorithms are a common first step in the solution process of optimiza-
tion problems, they showed a limited ability to scale with the complexity of the problems at
hand. Acceptable results were obtained in tasks of limited scale, but as the search space
and number of parameters increased the tested Local mutation approach struggled.
Self-Adaptation showed significant promise, allowing a neural network to quickly converge
to an optimum. However, the self-adaptation solutions tested had issues surpassing lo-
cal optima and the improvements provided over their non-adaptive variants were reduced
as the number of parameters of the network increased. Since mutation strength was
individually mutated for each parameter in the network, a large amount of parameters
attenuated the self adaptation. Still, these issues may be mitigated through different Self-
Adaptation approaches, as the ones implemented focused solely on parameter-wise mu-
tation strength tuning.
Initially, the Gaussian Mutation algorithm was implemented with the intention of being a
simple solution with little artifice. The only major difference introduced to this solution com-
pared to others, the probability of mutation, resulted in above expectation performance in
certain experiments, namely Cartpole, where the ability to perform more mutations per
generation was an advantage.
Lastly, the (1+1) Neuroevolution Algorithm showed consistently good results throughout
all experiments. The use of a heavy-tailed random distribution as the determining factor
of the mutation strength was a universally successful approach. The large probability of
low mutation strengths allows it to find precise optima with each, and the small but still
significant chance of large mutations resulted in the algorithm being able to surpass local
optima, traverse plateaus and successfully manage high parameter environments.
5.1
Future Work
As the timeline for this project was limited, a few natural courses of study were cut down.
At the same time, as development progressed and the results were analyzed, several
promising avenues of study were identified.
This project did not make use of several common techniques used in state-of-the-art Neu-
roevolutionary Algorithms. Neural Networks with activation functions should be the first
step of further work, as in many current applications of Machine Learning their non-linear
attributes are necessary. Additionally, methods like Crossover and Adaptive Topologies
are becoming more common, so further formal experimentation should be done to ascer-
tain their strengths.
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
31
==PAGE:39==
As the scale of digitalization increases, so does the scale of the solutions used. The
most notable applications of Machine Learning nowadays, like ChatGPT, make use of in-
credibly large neural networks with billions of neurons and trillions of connections. While
experiments of this magnitude are unlikely to be possible, an effort should still be made
to evaluate larger scale networks.
Our results showed potential in using different mutation probability values. Experimenta-
tion with a variety of values is a simple next step, without increasing complexity, that could
generate useful knowledge.
Moreover, Self-Adaptation showed merit. While the Self-Adaptive Mutation Strength mod-
ule tested had some shortcomings, this technique could be used for a variety of other
aspects of Neuroevolution:
• Self-adjusting mutation probabilities;
• Self-adaptive distributions, reflecting the ”novelty” parameter used in reinforcement
learning, to balance the exploration and exploitation dichotomy. By flatting a random
distribution from which mutation strengths are drawn, exploration can be promoted,
while moving towards an exponentially-bounded tail distribution can enhance ex-
ploitation;
In this paper we established how different algorithms can excel in different problem con-
texts. With this in mind, combination of multiple approaches could be a promising line of
work. This concept is already used in other areas of algorithms, like sorting algorithms. By
first surveying the environment (e.g. problem at hand, network in use) the best approach
can be selected and adapted as the execution progresses.
5.2
Limitations
A major limitation in the development of this project was execution time. Rust was chosen
as the programming language with this in mind, but larger experiments still took a signifi-
cant amount of time to complete. While all the main goals of the project were completed,
several of the topics described in the previous section could have been developed in this
time frame. In spite of that, adding more variables to test increases experiment running
time exponentially, so few emerging ideas could be implemented and analysed.
The programming language of choice was also a major decision that shaped the direction
of this thesis. As mentioned, Rust was initially selected due to its low resource use and
fast execution but the limited landscape of machine learning frameworks in libraries in
Rust only became apparent after significant development had been made. We planned
to make use of a widespread Machine Learning framework, like TensorFlow or PyTorch,
as these are available for Rust. However, these are implemented in such a way that the
direct control required by this project was not possible, and the decision to either repeat
all implementation made up to that point, or implement the neural network architecture
from scratch had to be made. Ultimately, due to the relatively simple features necessary
for this paper’s goals and the aforementioned performance gains, we chose to continue
with Rust.
32
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:40==
It’s uncertain what differences would come from moving to Python for its robust Machine
Learning landscape, but this was a major decision point in the development of the project.
We valued the potential to run more extensive experiments due to better performance
over gains in early development time from having access to a already developed Machine
Learning tools.
The graphical user interface was developed only for demonstration purposes and currently
displays solutions in plain text. With another week of development time, visualizations of
the solutions, like the vectors generated in a Sphere Problem or a video of the Cartpole
solution across time steps, could have been implemented. Since this thesis focused on
the implementation and research of Neuroevolution, this feature was not prioritized.

nals of Mathematical Statistics 43.2 (1972), pp. 645–646. DOI: 10 . 1214 / aoms /
1177692644. URL: https://doi.org/10.1214/aoms/1177692644.
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
35
==PAGE:43==
36
Implementation and Evaluation of Neuroevolution Algorithms
==PAGE:44==
A
User Manual
For the purpose of demonstrating the work developed in this thesis, a simple graphical
user interface was built.
When opening the program, a page with a variety of options is shown. These include:
1. General Parameters: Parameters required for all experiments implemented. This
includes the shape of the neural network, a resolution parameter used for discretiza-
tion and from which the maximum generation is set, the number of times the selected
experiment should be ran, and the optimum at which an execution is considered a
success. If desired, the user can also export the results into a Comma Separated
Value file;
2. Problem Parameters:
Parameters respective to the problem to be tested. The
user should select what problem using the dropdown menu. When relevant for the
chosen problem, other fields will be enabled (e.g. number of points for the Discrete
Hyper-sphere Problems);
3. Algorithm Parameters: Parameters respective to the algorithm to be tests. Once
again, the user can select the algorithm to use through the dropdown menu. If
required, other fields will be enabled, allowing customization of parameters like Self-
Adaptation parameters.
Once all the desired variables are set, simply click the ”Run Experiments” button. As each
iteration is finished, its results will be displayed in the text box at the bottom. If an export
file is set, these results will also be exported for further analysis.
